*9.4.*Discussion The main result of this work is an exact computation of the critical
index from self-consistency of the Minimal Area solution of the Loop Equa-tions. With correct recurrent equations (9.32), the contact terms needed for consistency of surface integrals of the vorticity correlation functions naturally arise from second functional derivatives of the regularized area ((9.9)).
The ends start meeting in this exotic solution, which initially raised so much confusion 26 years ago. However, does it meet the numerical experiment?
188
Initially, the authors of that experiment10 interpreted their results for the log-log derivative
*О»(p)*= d log
гҖҲ |О“|p
гҖү d log
вҲҡ AC
(9.56)
as some bi-fractal model, switching from K41 value*О»(p)*= 4p 3 at p < 4
to another slope*О»(p)*вүҲ 1.16p at 4 вүӨ p вүӨ 10. The linear fit of their data is not perfect; one can see that the slope*О»(p)*
p is never a constant вҖ“ it is
greater than 4 3 at small p and steadily decreases.
According to our theory, it should reach*О»(p)вҶ’*p. The finite moments are not expected to obey the Area law вҖ“ this is an opposite limit of large О“ which corresponds to the saddle point О“0 in the moments integral after switching to ln О“ as integration variable
гҖҲ |О“|p
гҖү =
ЛҶ вҲһ
0 dО“О“pP(О“, C) вҲқ
О“p+1 0 P(О“0, C)
( вҲ’ (*О“0вҲӮО“0*
)2 ln P(О“0, C) )вҲ’ 1
2 (1 + . . . ); (9.57)
p + 1 +*О“0вҲӮО“0*ln (
P(О“0, C) ) = 0 (9.58)
Here . . . stand for higher corrections to the saddle point integration, related to higher derivatives
(*О“0вҲӮО“0*
)n ln P(О“0, C), n > 2 in Taylor expansion near the saddle point. For power-like ln P(О“, C) these corrections go in inverse powers of p.
The largest of two possible saddle points at positive and negative О“ determines the asymptotic behavior of the moments. Another saddle point provides exponential corrections at large p. We expect the positive saddle point to dominate, as it decreases slower in numerical experiments,10 so we only use the О“ > 0 integral.
This saddle point at large p would approach the region of large О“ where we expect Area law to hold. Technically it is simpler to treat О“0 as an independent large variable and the saddle point equation (9.58) as parametric equation for p(О“0). In that case, one can explicitly compute all the terms to any order in the WKB expansion around the saddle point.
In practice, such saddle point approximations may be very accurate even for small p, as the Stirling formula for the Gamma function shows.
189
This numerical luck explains the validity of the Area Law in a whole inertial range and even small moments.
Let us assume the following correction
*О»(p)вҶ’ 2Оұp*+*ОІ*ln p (9.59)
where the coefficient*ОІ*is not universal and can depend on the shape of the loop and the area.19
The p dependence can be compared with experimental data20 at maximal available Reynolds number 1300. The following parameters fit the data at p = 3, ...10 with adjusted R2 = 0.999991 which means extremely well:
*Оұ*= 0.49Вұ 0.02 (9.60)*ОІ*= 0.92Вұ 0.01 (9.61)
We performed statistical analysis with MathematicaВ®. Let us compare with experiment our asymptotic formula for the ratio*О»(p)*
p . (see Fig. 40). As we can see, the green curve passes within error bars through all the data points at p = 3, ...10.
For those who do not believe in fitting, here is the plot of pure experimental data with error bars for*О»(p)вҲ’p*
ln p in a wider range of p including small p where we do not expect it to be constant.
It is consistent with the assumption of a constant limit, take a piece of paper and place its edge across the data points.
We take it as another validation of the Area Law. However, more experiments at higher Reynolds numbers would be needed to verify asymptotic index*Оұ*= 1
2 with two or more significant digits. The ratio*О»(p)*p
is clearly falling up to p = 10 and would keep falling after that as ln p p
according to our current fit. So, what happens with the experiment for p = 11, ...20?
If we start believing the Minimal Surface, we have to try and see how the correlation between two circulations О“1, О“2 around planar loops in

**10. Area Law**
This section is based on a recent paper published in the вҖҷ20-ties, after the DNS10 confirmed area law.

*10.2.*Stokes Condition and Mean Curvature Let us study this amazing duality of Minimal Surface to the turbulent
flow. First of all, why minimal surface? No string theory would require this minimal surface to arise as a classical solution (or at least we do not know any well-defined string theory equivalent to turbulence despite some interesting observations55).
The Stokes condition ((10.6)) is satisfied by the minimum condition. When the surface changes into SвҖІ, the linear variation reduces by volume Stokes theorem to Лӣ
SвҖІвҲ’S*dПғi(r)*
*ОҙU[C] ОҙПғi(r)*

(10.10)
with*ОҙV*being infinitesimal volume between SвҖІ and S. This linear variation must vanish by the definition of the minimal surface for the regularized area and its local limit.
The area derivative of the Minimal Area in regularized form, then, as before, reduces to the elimination of one integration
*ОҙAC*
*ОҙПғi(r)*= 2 ЛҶ

(*вҲ’ПҖ*

) (10.11)
Where ni(rМғ) is the local normal vector to the minimal surface at the nearest surface point rМғ to the 3d point r, and rвҠҘ is the component normal to the surface at rМғ. With this regularization, area derivative is defined everywhere in space but exponentially decreases away from the surface. Exactly at the surface, it reduces to twice the normal unit vector.

*10.4.*Exact Solution for Flat Loop Here is the biggest news of this paper: the minimal surface**exactly**

We also claim that for our solution, all higher moments are proportional to the powers of the area for the flat loop, though this is hard to verify directly, as we do not know the exact form of higher velocity correlations.
Note, however, that the same logarithmic velocity correlator in 3D space, as required to compute the second moment for a non-planar loop, will no longer produce*Оҙ*functions for vorticity correlations. We may use the Stokes theorem and integrate it over some curved surface, but the vorticity correlatorгҖҲ
*Пүi(r)Пүj(rвҖІ)*гҖү

our solution does not imply a short-range correlation of vorticity, just that it has a scaling dimension вҲ’1.
An interesting property of our solution is that it leaves the scaling function arbitrary, as long as it depends upon the minimal area. The dependence of higher vorticity correlations in a circulation background is uniquely expressed in the basic PDF scaling function, but this function remains arbitrary.
We shall accept the solution for the planar loop and try to generalize it for the non-planar one.
*10.5.*Loop Equation in Quadratic approximation Let us introduce a local quadratic approximation to a surface, as before
(with K1 = K, K2 = вҲ’K), and n(rвҖІ) given by (10.13), with z direction being the local normal vector at the contour. The curvature K refers to the integration point r.
As we found the minimal surface in Appendix H in7 the conformal coordinates at the boundary are consistent with these x, y in quadratic approximation: x goes along the local tangent direction of the loop, and y goes inside the surface. As for z it goes along the normal to the surface n(r). We take r as an origin. The loop C expands as a piece of parabola in xy plane:
C1 = x (10.30)

Now, the Stokes condition will, as before, be satisfied in virtue of minimality. Still, it will be interesting to derive a replacement of the mean curvature equation for the ordinary minimal surface. Repeating the above computations in the local tangent plane, we find here:
K1 + K2 =*ni(x)вҲӮiПҶ(r)~r=~R(x)*(10.40)
On the left, we have mean curvature at the surface, and on the right, we have the normal derivative of the conformal metric field projected at the surface.
This property means that we can keep the ordinary minimal surface, with K1 + K2 = 0, and ensure that the conformal metric varies only along the surface but not in the normal direction. In the local tangent plane, this means that*ПҶ(x,*y, z) =*ПҶ(x,*y, 0), so that it changes only in a local tangent plane but not in the normal direction.
Remember, this is just the Stokes condition, not yet the loop equation, but the net result is that we keep the minimal surface in the sense of being the surface of the minimal area in ordinary induced metric. However, the Extended Area*AC[ПҶ]*has an extra factor*exp(ПҶ(r))*which varies along the surface.
For the loop equation to hold, the following condition must be valid:Лӣ C

Thus we get closed set of integro-differential equations for parametric equation of surface ri = Ri(u, v) and the external conformal metric*ПҶ(u,*v) which is two dimensional vector field. So, we have 2-dimensional surface embedded in 4-dimensional space x, y, z,*ПҶ.*

205
*10.8.*Conclusion The main result of this section is the exact solution of the loop equation
for an arbitrary flat loop, which includes a 2D loop equation. With scaling index*Оұ*= 1
2 as derived in the previous work, the minimal area solves the loop equations and passes the known tests for the circulation moments. For the general, non-planar loop, the equation for the minimal surface stays the same (mean curvature equals zero), and we derive an integral equation ((10.45)) for the self-consistent conformal metric. The correlation scaling functions stay the same, and local vorticity in expectation values is still directed along the normal to the minimal surface. The coordinate dependence of vorticity correlations gets modified by extra factors of conformal metric*exp(ПҶ).*In other words, we have a 2d-surface in 4-dimensional space x, y, z,*ПҶ*with the dependence of the x, y, z coordinates given by the ordinary minimal surface, and the last coordinate changing along this minimal surface in case this surface is curved.
**11. Recent progress of the Kelvinon Solution and Circulation PDF**
*11.1.*Time evolution of the Kelvinon We have recently clarified the meaning and the basic features of the
domain wall solution of the loop equation, and its relation to the Kelvin theorem and GBF.
The Kelvinon velocity field with some Clebsch winding numbers n, m, which we studied in the section 7, represents a class of time-dependent solution of the Euler equation. The external force ~f is assumed fixed in this solution, to be averaged later.
The loop equation also has a particular solution in the WKB limit
ОЁnm[C,*Оі]*=
гҖҲгҖҲ exp
( Дұ*Оі*
Лӣ C ~vnm В· d~r
)гҖү t
гҖү f
=
гҖҲ exp*(4ПҖmДұ ОіZ)*
гҖү f (11.1)
where гҖҲгҖү f is the averaging over the Gaussian distribution of constant vector ~f with variance*Пғ*and гҖҲгҖүt is the time averaging for the evolution according to Clebsch Flow Equation.
206
The Kelvinon normalization constant Z depends upon ~f through the energy balance equation (7.40). It is time-independent in the Clebsch Flow Equation.
The exponential of circulation of any stationary solution of the Euler equation would satisfy the WKB limit of the loop equation by construction. We have seen it on the example of the tensor area law solution of the loop equation, corresponding to the uniform global vorticity solution of the Navier-Stokes equation (inadequate to the turbulence problem).
We, however, are looking for something else in the loop equation. We need time-independence of the velocity circulation over a**particular**stationary loop C.
For this conservation of circulation, it suffices to have a time-dependent solution of the Clebsch Flow Equation (7.36), satisfying the following boundary conditions.
The velocity field in this solution must be tangent to the discontinuity surface and aligned with the bounding loop C.
As we have seen, these boundary conditions follow from the balance of most singular terms in the Clebsch Flow Equation.
The Clebsch Flow Equation resolves the ambiguity of the Euler equations for the Clebsch field.
The loop functional will be time independent in virtue of these boundary conditions, in agreement with the Kelvin theorem, as we have seen in the section7.
There are topological charges (7.29),(7.33). These charges are properties of the solution rather than extra conditions.
These charges are conserved by the time evolution by the Clebsch Flow Equation, as this continuous evolution preserves the topology.
We have to start this evolution from the Clebsch field with a particular topology and then let it evolve according to the Clebsch Flow Equation.
The explicit examples of the Clebsch field with the required topology were presented in (7.2).
The time average of any loop functional made of a solution of the Clebsch Flow Equation with these examples as initial conditions will provide the desired solution of the steady loop equation.
Thus, our Kelvinon does not belong to GBF. It is adjusted to the loop C by the requirement that C bounds its discontinuity surface.
Without this requirement, our circulation would not satisfy the loop equation, nor will it be time-independent.
207
In general case CвҖІ 6= C, this circulation would reduce to the area inside some loop at the Clebsch sphere S2
Лӣ CвҖІ ~vnm В· d~r = Z
Лӣ*Оі*
*ПҶ1dПҶ2*=*ZArea(Оі)*(11.2)
where*Area(Оі)*is the area on S2 outside the loop*Оі*= ~S(CвҖІ) mapped by the Clebsch field on this sphere.
By the Kelvin theorem, this area is always conserved in Euler dynamics for the**liquid**loop, moving with the flow. For a static loop, it is not conserved in general.
Our flow for Clebsch field is not steady; it evolves according to Clebsch Flow Equation. However, as long as the singularity surface stays steady, as we assume, the circulation is conserved, which is all we need to validate the steady loop equation.
The curve*ОіC*= ~S(C) as we have seen, in virtue of our boundary conditions for the Clebsch field S(C) = (0, 0, 1), reduces to an infinitesimal circle around the North pole of S2, which led to the external area being the whole area*4ПҖ*of the sphere times the winding number m.
The constant Z is determined as a function of the force ~f from the energy balance condition (7.40). Again, this is not the extra condition but rather a consequence of the full Navier-Stokes equation for steady state. Later, we take the limit of*ОҪвҶ’*0 and leave the main terms.
Other than this approximation, this ОЁnm represents an exact solution in the limit of*ОҪвҶ’*0.
*11.2.*Kelvinon loop functionals and Gaussian forces As we have found in the previous section 7 (see also Appendix E), there
is the topologically stable Kelvinon solution ~vK(~r) of the Euler equations in Clebsch variables a the sphere S2 (K.L.S. domain wall bounded by the Alice string).
The loop functional ОЁ(C) for this solution represents the Gaussian average over the random force ~f of the phase factor exp (Дұ*О“C/ОҪ)*for the circulation О“C =

with ~вҲҮt being the tangent gradient, Sdisc(C) is the discontinuity surface for the Kelvinon, and Snn is the local normal strain. гҖҲгҖүt stands for averaging over the time evolution of the Clebsch field according to Clebsch Flow Equation.
The shape of discontinuity surface S :*вҲӮS*= C is so far arbitrary. The vorticity at the surface is normal to it, just as required by the loop equation.
The velocity circulation around the loop C is О“C =*4ПҖZm*(see (7.29)). In its initial form, О“C is a Stokes type functional of the loop C, which
can be varied by area at any point~r in space, producing vorticity ~вҲҮГ—~vK(~r). The continuation from the surface to the whole space R3 is provided by the Kelvinon solution Sa(~r).
The positive definite functional ОЈ(C) represents the surface enstrophy coming from vorticity*~Пү*= eabcSa~вҲҮSb Г— ~вҲҮSc (without the Z factor). This vorticity is concentrated within the local width h (~r вҲҲ Sdisc) on the discontinuity surface Sdisc(C) bounded by C.
The fact that О“C represents a velocity circulation over a steady edge of a steady discontinuity surface of the solution of the Euler equation (with fixed external force ~f ) automatically makes it a solution of the loop equation in the turbulent limit, as we discussed in the previous section.
The averaging over Gaussian force can be interchanged with the loop operators.
209
Therefore, this ОЁ(C) satisfies the Euler loop equation. The same is, of course, true about any other superposition of exponents of circulation of Euler solution, but this one has a physical meaning we need.
The energy balance relation between Z and ~f follows from the steady solution of the Navier-Stokes equation in the turbulent limit, which we discussed above, in section 6.
This relation is not an external constraint; it was derived by taking the time derivative of the fluid energy 1
2
Вҙ R3
d3r~v2, integrating by parts
and taking boundary conditions at infinity, where the constant force ~f is pumping in the energy, to be dissipated at the vortex sheet of our Kelvinon.
We used the energy balance relation from the full Navier-Stokes equation in the limit of*ОҪвҶ’*0 (anomalous energy dissipation) and applied it to the normalization parameter Z of the Euler solution for Clebsch field.
This modification of the Euler dynamics corresponds to the principle we suggested in the previous section 4.2: to consider the Euler dynamics as a limit*ОҪвҶ’*0 of the Navier-Stokes dynamics.
This principle leads to stability conditions, selecting the solutions of the Euler equation. It also leads to anomalous dissipation 4.67, which normalizes the arbitrary area*4ПҖZ*of the sphere for the compactified Clebsch field in the weak steady Euler solution.
We took advantage of the superposition principle for the linear loop equation (SchrГ¶dinger equation in loop space in our quantum interpretation).
In the next section, we shall use this superposition principle to sum over topological classes.
*11.3.*Kelvinon and the Minimal Surface With arbitrary discontinuity surface, this О“ is a functional of the loop
**and the shape of discontinuity surface.**This surface defines the solution for the Clebsch Flow Equations given angular discontinuities*2ПҖn, 2ПҖm*at this surface, around the loop C, and vanishing normal derivative of Clebsch field.
Therefore, the circulation implicitly depends on the discontinuity surface through the Kelvinon field.
The analysis of the WKB limit of the loop equation in the previous section 10.6 showed that the solution could depend on the following functional of the loop and some scalar field*ПҒ(~r)*(conformal external
210
metric)
*AC[ПҒ]*=
ЛҶ ~rвҲҲSdisc

) ; (11.4)
*вҲӮnПҒ*(~r вҲҲ Sdisc) = K1 + K2 (11.5)
where K1, K2 are the main local curvatures of the surface. Suppose we express this conformal metric locally through the tangent
gradients of the Clebsch field. Its normal derivative will vanish due to the Neumann boundary condition for the Kelvinon field at the minimal surface.
This relation will lead to the extremum condition of the ordinary area of our surface, independently of the external conformal metric, as long as it obeys the same Neumann boundary conditions as the Kelvinon field.
Now we compare this form of the functional*AC[ПҒ]*with our Kelvinon solution, and we find the correspondence

*11.4.*The algebraic solution for the loop functional Thus we have found an explicit analytic solution for the circulation
PDF, solving the loop equation and corresponding to the K.L.S. domain wall shaped as the minimal surface bounded by the loop C.

from10 in the previous section 7.9. The fit was as good as it gets, which strongly supported the existence of these Kelvinons.
We can go one step further and study the effects of the time reversal breaking in the Kelvinon solution.

*11.5.*PDF tails and time symmetry breaking The asymptotic tails of the PDF can be easiest to compute from the
integral representation

The saddle point computation chooses one of the six saddle points in the complex plane.

Let us first consider the case of positive О“. The relevant point in this case is {
tвҶ’ вҲҡ

We observe the explicit breaking of the time-reversal symmetry in the turbulent region |О“| *ОҪ.*This breaking comes from the time-odd winding number m of the Kelvinon. With the anti-Kelvinon, we would have the PDF reflected О“вҮ’ вҲ’О“.
We have the Stokes phenomenon from the point of view of the loop equation analysis. Two different analytic functions give the asymptotic behavior at the positive and negative О“.
This phenomenon resolves the paradox of Sasha Polyakov, who objected to the early version of the Area law on the following grounds. The Minimal area is invariant against the orientation reversal of the loop, as opposed to circulation, which changes its sign. The PDF must be an even function of the ratio of О“ to any function of the minimal area.
We now see two asymptotic solutions to the loop equation: one for positive and another for negative circulation.
We also see that for the non-planar loop C, the dependence of its shape is more complex than just the minimal area, though the discontinuity surface still coincides with the minimal surface.
The orientation reversal does not continue one asymptotic solution into another because of the Stokes phenomena in our integral representation (11.13).
213

*11.7.*The circulation moments in Kelvinon solution By construction, the Kelvinon solution only applies to high moments
corresponding to the tails of the PDF. In particular, the first moment is finite in this solution, whereas in the DNS, it is subtracted.

It becomes an important challenge to measure higher moments at large Reynolds numbers. Does the effective*О·(n)/n*get closer to 1? At the DNS in10 at n вҲј 10 it was about 1.1 and falling.
We can only predict leading terms, as the next correction will come from the higher WKB corrections.
We conclude that there are no multi-fractal laws in the Kelvinon solution. Asymptotically, at large moments, we have linear trajectory*О·(n)вҶ’*n. At smaller n, and in particular, at n = 3, the moments are no longer determined by Kelvinon. The asymptotic line*О·(n)*= n does not cross the exact K41 value*О·(3)*= 4. There is an intermediate regime at finite n, as it was suggested in.10
We do not see any theoretical grounds for conformal field theory here (for example, it would predict the scaling dimension of velocity field dвҲ’ 1 = 2 instead of observed вүҲ вҲ’1
3). The intermediate moments could have a more complex dependence on the loop size than the pure power law with any exponent.
There are scale-dependent corrections from the higher order terms of the WKB expansion to logarithmic derivatives of the moments by the scale, which could be interpreted as scale-dependent logarithmic derivatives of the moments*О·(n,*r) =*вҲӮ*
*вҲӮ*log r log гҖҲ (О“вҲ’ гҖҲО“гҖү)nгҖү.
*11.8.*Sum over topologies and quantum equivalence Here is an important observation. The loop equation is a**linear**
SchrГ¶dinger equation in loop space, even though the original Navier-Stokes and Euler equations are nonlinear.
There is no superposition principle in fluid dynamics, but there is one in quantum mechanics in loop space.
219
We already used this superposition principle when we took the averaging over the random forces out of the loop functional. The average of the loop functionals, each satisfying the loop equation in virtue of the classical Euler equation with fixed force ~f , also represented a solution to the loop equation.
Now we apply the same argument to the topological classes. In virtue of the superposition principle for the linear equation, the
general solution would be a superposition for the loop functional as well as for its Fourier transform, вҖ“ the PDF
### ОЁ[C,*Оі]*= вҲ‘
nвҲ’ 1
**2вҲҲZ,mвҲҲZ**
An,mОЁnm[C,*Оі]*=
### вҲ‘
nвҲ’ 1
**2вҲҲZ,mвҲҲZ**
AnmвҲҡ det
( 1вҲ’ Дұ*Оі 2mПғЕңвҲ’1*
n2ОЈ(C)
) ; (11.33)
### О [C, О“] = вҲ‘
nвҲ’ 1
**2вҲҲZ,mвҲҲZ**
An,mО nm[C, О“] (11.34)
What are the weights Anm? At the moment, we cannot say, other than demand, that these weights must add up to 1. This problem remains for future research.
Here are some general considerations. The solution of the Navier-Stokes equation is supposedly unique in the
absence of forcing and at fixed initial data. There is even a Millennium Prize offered for proving or (for the sake of caution) disproving this widely accepted conjecture.
There is, though, an instability at a large enough Reynolds number (small enough viscosity compared to a typical velocity circulation).
In this case of infinitesimal viscosity, infinitesimal random forces at the boundary are known to lead to stochastic behavior, making the whole question of uniqueness of the solution of the Navier-Stokes equation moot. The real turbulence problem is: what manifold is covered by this stochastic motion and what is the nature of spontaneous breaking of time reversal invariance in the Euler equation?
Coming back to the sum over topologies, let us consider the time evolution of the Navier-Stokes flow. There was a deterministic flow at the initial moment, far from any fixed points. According to our scenario, it
220
approaches one of the stable fixed points at some time, corresponding to certain topological charges of the Clebsch field.
By definition of the winding numbers, no continuous deformation can change them. However, in our system, we assumed some random external boundary forces ~f , supplying the energy dissipated at high velocity gradients.
With a certain probability, these random forces can change topology as they violate the continuity of the solution. Moreover, an enhancement effect caused by large vorticity in vortex sheets leads to finite results of infinitesimal forcing at infinitesimal viscosity.
In that case, the flow will jump between topological classes, approach the fixed point in each class and cover the vicinity of this fixed point (which is, in fact, a fixed manifold).
In that picture, the coefficients Amn are time frequencies (or probabilities) of Clebsch field getting into the corresponding topological class as a result of Gaussian random forcing.
Naturally, there is no sum over histories in our classical theory. The Navier-Stokes flow does not go over various alternatives with the weight exp
( Дұ S
*ОҪ*
) at the same moment.
There are rather some dynamical instabilities triggered by external forcing, resulting in a weighted sum of contributions from various topological classes to the time average of the PDF over infinite time.
The turbulent flow spends some fraction of time in each of these classes, jumping from one to another, triggered by (unlikely) large fluctuations of the random force.
From that interpretation, it follows that all Amn > 0, unlike quantum mechanics, where the eigenfunctions could add to wave function with complex or negative coefficients.
**12. Conclusions and discussion**
After 35 years of blind trials and errors, we are beginning to see the light.
*12.1.*Clebsch confinement and compactification phase transition We have presented theoretical arguments and strong support from the
DNS in favor of Kelvinons underlying the intermittency phenomena for the velocity circulation in strong turbulence.
221
In the Introduction, we stated that the ultimate goal of turbulence studies was to find the solution of the Navier-Stokes equations in the limit of vanishing viscosity and explain why and how it covers some manifold instead of staying unique given Cauchy data.
In this review paper, summarizing (and debugging!) our recent re-search12, 13, 8, 17, 18, 19, 9, 20, 21 we describe our conjecture for this manifold and the way it is covered.
While trying to achieve this goal, we encountered ambiguity in the Euler equation, which is the limit of the Navier-Stokes we are studying. The Euler equation represents a Hamiltonian system in Clebsch variables. However, the topology of the target space for these variables remains arbitrary.
We adopted the sphere S2, which is one of the possibilities. Another would be R2, in which case there will be no topological numbers and no Kelvinons. We conclude that R2 is appropriate for the weak turbulent flow, and S2 вҖ“ for the strong turbulent one.
**Thus, we conjecture that there is a compactification phase transition**R2 вҮ’ S2**for the Clebsch field, like that in a string theory.**
The weak turbulence39 has this Clebsch field propagating as plane waves and leading to the K41 energy spectrum in weak turbulence.
In strong turbulence, there are no Clebsch waves: this is a geometric phase, with Clebsch confined to a sphere in the target space.
The velocity circulation in our theory is proportional to the winding number of the Clebsch field satisfying the Clebsch Flow Equation (the Euler equation for Clebsch field with extra incompressible motion in target space S2 in addition to passive flow in a self-parametrized velocity field).
The time evolution of the Clebsch field described by Clebsch Flow Equation is not steady, but it is compact, as the Clebsch field is confined to a sphere S2.
Here is our answer to the question posed in the Introduction: the Clebsch field covers S2 driven by the Clebsch Flow Equation. This motion is more complex than a uniform covering of this sphere. Some nontrivial topology is involved, with two winding numbers n вҲҲ**Z**+ 1
2 , m вҲҲ**Z.**The circulation О“C is related to the integral of the Euler motion Z as
О“C =*4ПҖmZ.*The infinitesimal tube around the loop C in physical space R3 is mapped to the torus in Clebsch target space with the area*8ПҖ2nmZ.*((7.33)).
222
*12.2.*Matching the DNS The parameters depend upon the loop shape C through the time
average of a surface integral (11.3) over the minimal surface SC of some function expressed in terms of the Kelvinon solution for the Clebsch field ~S вҲҲ S2.
This winding number is time-odd, which explains the asymmetry of the circulation PDF. The left and the right asymptotic behavior (11.14),(11.15) was perfectly fitted to the DNS.10
It is shown at (11.18),(11.19) and displayed at Fig.44, Fig. 45. The time-symmetry breaking is just 5%, but it is well beyond statistical error 0.1%. The ratio of fitted exponential decrements is*ОІ*вүҲ 0.95.
The circulation moments for odd powers are negative, in agreement with DNS. The quantitative comparison must wait for the future DNS with larger order moments.
In principle, the higher moments add no information to the tails of the PDF, being related to these tails by the saddle point approximation of the one-dimensional integral for the moments at high order.
The solution of the Clebsch Flow Equation for the Kelvinon field for the simplest loops, such as a circle and rectangle, should be computed for initial data for the flat circle. These solutions would allow computation of the dependence of the Kelvinon dissipation ОЈ(C) as a functional of the loop shape.
*12.3.*Extreme intermittency and its microscopic mechanism The exponential decay of the velocity circulation distribution, discov-
ered in the DNS in,10 and explained in this work, represents an extreme intermittency phenomenon.
The deviations of probability distributions in Statistical Physics from the Gaussian laws belong to the Critical Phenomena, extensively studied in the previous century, culminating with the Renormalization Group and Conformal bootstrap.
These methods apply to**local**statistical systems, such as ferromagnetic near the Curie point or liquid Helium near the lambda point. The starting point of this Critical phenomena theory was a Gibbs distribution at a microscopic scale. The Renormalization group described the transformation of this distribution when the microscopic degrees of freedom are "integrated out" or averaged in the Gibbs partition function.
223
Researchers have tried to apply the same methods to the Turbulence problem for many years without much success. Turbulence is not a usual statistical system with a given Gibbs distribution; the first problem is to find a substitute for this distribution in a dissipative system, where energy is not conserved.
Another complication is the lack of locality in incompressible fluid dynamics. Due to incompressibility conditions, pressure instantly redistributes the velocity field in the whole space. Long-range correlations and extended vortex structures explicitly violate the locality in turbulence.
Therefore, a new physical mechanism is responsible for the deviation of the Gaussian distribution of the velocity circulation. This circulation by the Stokes theorem equals the surface integral of normal vorticity of a surface bounded by the loop. If the vorticity elements at this surface were random and independent, this circulation would become a Gaussian variable by the**CLT.**
It is decaying much slower, as an exponential, which manifests intermittency. It tells us that these strongly correlated elementary vorticities at the surface are part of a coherent structure with no or very little fluctuations. We argue that this structure is a vortex sheet with some normal vorticity at the surface.
This vortex sheet must satisfy some dynamical equation analogous to kinetic equations leading us to the Gibbs-Boltzmann distribution. Our theory says this equation is the loop equation, and its steady solution in extreme turbulent (i.e., WKB) limit is the Kelvinon.
In that sense, our turbulence theory is simpler than the Critical phenomena, as it is described by a single classical Clebsch Flow Equation, leading to an algebraic solution for the loop functional.
*12.4.*Kelvinon as the analog of Gibbs distribution The Newton dynamics covers the energy surface due to the energy
conservation, which leads to the ergodic hypothesis and the Gibbs distribution exp
(*вҲ’ОІE*
) . In our theory, the conserved circulation of the Kelvinon
plays a similar role to the Gibbs energy. Regardless of how the Clebsch field covers the sphere S2 at infinite
time, the circulation stays constant within each topological class at the fixed realization of the random force.
We explicitly constructed the initial data for the Clebsch field with specific winding numbers. Given these initial data and the usual boundary
224
condition of vanishing normal derivatives to the discontinuity surface, the solution is specified up to arbitrary gauge function*h(ПҶ1, ПҶ2).*
There could be a fixed point in the Clebsch Flow Equation, like the ergodic hypothesis for NewtonвҖҷs motion on the energy surface, but we no longer need this assumption.
Our PDF for the velocity circulation only involves the time averages of certain space integrals depending on the time-dependent solution of this equation. We need to assume, of course, the existence of these averages over infinite time.
Using energy balance arguments, we computed the turbulent limit of the loop functional
гҖҲ exp (Дұ*ОіО“C)*
гҖү , averaged over Gaussian external forces.
This functional turned out to be an algebraic function of*Оі*(11.8). Here is where we bypassed the ergodic hypothesis. The unique topol-
ogy of the Kelvinon allowed us to relate the distribution of circulation to certain time averages of this unknown compact motion on the Clebsch sphere.
*12.5.*Duality The loop equation (beyond the strong turbulence WKB approximation)
for the loop functional has the form of the SchrГ¶dinger equation in loop space with complex Hamiltonian.
This mysterious relation between classical probability and the Fourier integral of the quantum wave function over*Пү*= 1
hМ„ needs yet to be fully understood. This relation leads to "quantum effects," such as sum over topologies, which are observable (in principle).
The Kelvinon fixed point of the loop equation corresponds to the leading term of the WKB expansion, which goes in**positive**fractional powers of viscosity.
In terms of modern QFT, we have the**duality**between fluctuating velocity field and fluctuating geometry of the Clebsch field, mapping the physical space R3 onto the sphere S2.
The strong turbulence corresponds to this dual geometric theoryвҖҷs weak (i.e., classical) phase. The complete theory would allow us to compute higher terms of this WKB expansion. This computation is a hard technical problem, but now it is within our reach once the loop equations are well defined and the leading classical term is identified.
There are examples of such duality in low-dimensional flows. The Burgers equation describes 1D compressible flow. It is dual to the statistics
225
of shocks,52, 53, 54 which are 1D versions of our vortex sheets. Topology in the 1D case is much more primitive than in 3D, but this
duality still holds. These shocks are responsible for the intermittency in the Burgers equation, which inspired us to look for topological defects in 3D Euler.
ADS/CFT duality in the conformal field theories is quite familiar to the modern theoretical physicists specializing in QFT, String theory, and even Solid State Physics.
In turbulence, duality is something unknown, which sounds like a dangerous heresy. There could be only one physical picture, they say, and it was given to us by the prophet Kolmogorov. We can only elaborate on this picture and build models.
This uniqueness of physical picture is very common in Physics. How-ever, on some rare lucky occasions, the universal physical phenomena can be described by more than one language and by more than one mathematical structure.
We mentioned velocity-shock duality in the Burgers equation and the ADS/CFT duality in Quantum Field Theory.
There was a third story of unexpected duality, which we know from personal experience.58 The 2D quantum gravity, as we now know it, can be described either by Liouville theory (continuum CFT) or by the random set of adjacent triangles, counted using random matrix theory and the theory of orthogonal polynomials.
It took years of shouting from rooftops to convince the gravity community that their hard problem of solving the Liouville theory could be exactly solved using nineteenth-century mathematics.
In the case of the loop equations and vortex sheets, it took already 35 years to develop the idea of duality between fluctuating velocity and fluctuating geometry. Just like it happened with Matrix models of Gravity, nobody believed in these ideas while they were in development.
In the case of the Matrix Model of Quantum Gravity, the epiphany moment came when these two dual theories produced identical results for some critical indexes.
Perhaps, the perfect match of our predictions for the circulation PDF with the DNS,10 based on the conventional approach and compatible with Kolmogorov at intermediate Reynolds numbers, will have the same effect.
226
*12.6.*The local velocity and strain statistics The statistics of the velocity field is another story. In our theory, there is a significant difference between the potential
and rotational parts of the velocity field. In the Clebsch variables, vorticity is related to the Clebsch field by
local expression. In contrast, potential velocity (including the strain tensor) involves the Biot-Savart integral, effectively accumulating vorticity distribution from the infinite volume and averaging over its fluctuations.
This non-locality and averaging make the statistics of the potential part very different from that of the rotational part.
As we argue in the section 5.8, the local velocity statistics are Gaussian due to the accumulation of tails from Biot-Savart integrals from uncorrelated vorticity structures far away from each other.
The Central Limit theorem, in this case, would work, unlike the case of the PDF of large circulations, which aggregate vorticity from a coherent vortex structureвҖ“ the Kelvinon.
Moreover, the local strain*SОұОІ*= 1*2(вҲӮОұvОІ*+*вҲӮОІvОұ)*is expected to obey
the distribution of a Gaussian random symmetric tensor, well known in the Random Matrix theory, with an obvious constraint of vanishing trace (5.99).
This prediction can be verified in DNS, particularly the table of the moments, which we calculated analytically in (5.101), (5.103).
We used that distribution to compute the energy dissipation on a non-compact CVS vortex sheet in the section 5.9.
In the loop approach, the circulation PDF satisfies the closed equation, where the potential velocity part drops.
Moreover, the velocity correlations grow with distance, making us doubt the universality of the (potential part of) turbulent motion.
Why is the velocity fluctuation in the middle of the developed turbulent flow not influenced by the fluctuations near the boundary if their correlation function**grows**with separation?
These arguments do not apply so much to the vorticity fluctuations, which are at least decreasing.
The moments of the circulation (rotational part) are quite different from those of velocity differences (which mix both parts).
227
*12.7.*Heuristic model for the multi-fractal laws We still cannot quantitatively describe some phenomena observed in
DNS, such as apparent multi-fractal scaling laws for moments of velocity differences, first suggested by Parisi and Frisch in60 by (presumed) analogy with conformal field theory.
There is an impressive phenomenological theory by Sreenivasan and Yakhot,61, 62, 63 offering a model with a few parameters, accurately fitting the observed scaling indexes*О¶(n).*
These authors advocate the new phase transition to the "fourth state of turbulence," leading to saturation of the scaling indexes as functions of the order n of the moment.
In our approach, what they observe reflects the transition from weak to strong turbulence, originating in the compactification of the target space of the Clebsch variables.
These authors mostly considered the velocity differences rather than the velocity circulation we have studied, so there is no direct overlap between our theories.
Still, we can offer some heuristic explanations of the success of their model.
The turbulent flow is potential in most of the space, with vorticity collapsed into vortex sheets.
When two points measure velocity difference, with a certain small probability*Оө*1, a vortex sheet passes between them. With complementary probability*Оұ*= 1вҲ’*Оө*there will be a potential field, where the Bernoulli law holds p = вҲ’1
2~v 2.
In this case, the velocity difference is a linear separation function. With probability*Оө,*there will be a step function (velocity gap).
In,62, 63 the infinite chain of the Hopf relations for expectation values of powers of velocity differences was closed by introducing the "turbulent viscosity."As a result of this approximation, the pressure was replaced by pвҮ’ вҲ’1
2*Оұ~v2,*with*Оұ*вүҲ 0.95. Within our heuristics, this*Оұ*is the probability of finding no vortex
sheet between two points. After this closing, the relations for the velocity difference moments were nicely fitted62 to the DNS.
The microscopic theory of the potential part of turbulent velocity differences without any model approximations remains a problem for the future. In addition to fitting the DNS, this theory should give a space-
228
time picture of relevant flow and explain the physical nature of the phase transition to the strong turbulence.
We also desperately need large-scale DNS to verify the Kelvinon theoryвҖҷs predictions more accurately and investigate vorticityвҖҷs spatial distribution. We also need a confirmation of the Random Matrix predictions (5.101), (5.103) for the strain moments.
229

**Appendix D. Geometric structure of constrained vortex surfaces (by Camillo De Lellis, Elia BruГ©)**
246
This short note derives some geometric conditions that MigdalвҖҷs constrained vortex surfaces have to satisfy.
Appendix*D.1.*CVS conditions: geometric interpretation and instability in the compact case
Let S вҠӮ**R3**be a (sufficiently smooth) complete connected surface. We denote by SвҲ’ the region enclosed by S , and set S+ :=**R3**\ (S вҲӘ SвҲ’).
According to MigdalвҖҷs definition, a CVS (constrained vortex surface) solution to the Euler equations is a pair of (sufficiently smooth) velocity fields (vвҲ’, v+) defined on SвҲ’ and S+, respectively, such that
div v+ = div vвҲ’ = 0 (D.1) curl v+ = curl vвҲ’ = 0 (D.2)
v+ , vвҲ’ are tangent to S (D.3) (Dv+ + DvвҲ’) В· (v+ вҲ’ vвҲ’) = 0 (D.4)
|v+|2 вҲ’ |vвҲ’|2 is locally constant on S . (D.5)
Moreover, the constrained vortex surface has to satisfy the following stability condition
гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n , nгҖү < 0 , (D.6)
where n is the exterior normal to S and гҖҲa, bгҖү denotes the scalar product of the vectors a and b.
We have the following geometric characterization of (D.3), (D.4) and (D.5).
**Theorem Appendix D.1.**Conditions (D.3), (D.4), (D.5) are satisfied iff{ A(vвҲ’, vвҲ’) = A(v+, v+) [v+, vвҲ’] = 0 ,
(D.7)
where A is the second fundamental form of S , and [v+, vвҲ’] is the Lie bracket, i.e.
[v+, vвҲ’] = Dv+ В· vвҲ’ вҲ’ DvвҲ’ В· v+ .
**Remark Appendix D.2.**Recall that the Lie Bracket [v+, vвҲ’] = (Dv+ В· vвҲ’ вҲ’ DvвҲ’ В· v+)|S is tangent to S because v+ and vвҲ’ are tangent to S .
247
Proof. Condition (D.5) is equivalent to
(Dv+ В· v+ вҲ’ DvвҲ’ В· vвҲ’)|S is parallel to n . (D.8)
We rewrite (D.4) as I + I I = 0, where
I = Dv+ В· v+ вҲ’ DvвҲ’ В· vвҲ’ I I = вҲ’Dv+ В· vвҲ’ + DvвҲ’ В· v+ ,
and observe that I is normal to S , as a consequence of (D.8), while I I is tangent to S . Indeed,
гҖҲI I, nгҖү = A(v+, vвҲ’)вҲ’ A(vвҲ’, v+) = 0 .
In particular I + I I = 0 iff I = 0 and I I = 0. It is immediate to rewrite the two conditions as (D.7).
Regarding the stability condition (D.6), we can prove the following rigidity result.
**Theorem Appendix D.3.**If S вҠӮ**R3**is a smooth compact closed surface and v+, vвҲ’ a pair of vector fields that satisfy the conditions (D.1) and (D.3), then
ЛҶ S гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n , nгҖү = 0 . (D.9)
In particular, (D.6) cannot hold.
The same argument allows for a similar conclusion when S is a smooth compact surface with boundary*вҲӮS*and v++ vвҲ’ is tangent to the boundary.
**Theorem Appendix D.4.**Assume S вҠӮ**R3**is a smooth compact surface with smooth boundary*вҲӮS*and v+, vвҲ’ a pair of vector fields that satisfy the conditions (D.1) and (D.3). If in addition v+ + vвҲ’ is tangent to*вҲӮS*, then
ЛҶ S гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n , nгҖү = 0 . (D.10)
In particular, (D.6) cannot hold.
248
**Remark Appendix D.5.**Clearly Theorem Appendix*D.3*can be thought as the particular case of Theorem Appendix*D.4*has empty boundary. The smoothness needed by the proof given below is that the surface and its boundary are both C1
(i.e. they have a tangent at every point, which in turn varies continuosly), and that the vector fields are C1, i.e. continuously differentiable.
Proof of Theorem Appendix*D.4.*Fix a point p вҲҲ S and choose a local orthonormal frame around p consisting of e1, e2, n, where the vectors e1 and e2 are tangent to S . Note that, since both v+ and vвҲ’ are divergence free we have that D(v+ + vвҲ’) is a trace-free matrix, and thus
гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n, nгҖү = вҲ’гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· e1, e1гҖү вҲ’ гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· e2, e2гҖү . (D.11)
Let g be the Riemannian scalar product induced on S by the Euclidean one, and denote by вҲҮS the corresponding Levi-Civita connection. Recall that the latter coincides with the connection induced by the Euclidean connection. This amounts to say that, if X, Y and Z are tangent vector fields to S , then
g(вҲҮSY X, Z) = гҖҲDX В·Y, ZгҖү .
If we apply this to Y = Z = ei and X = v+ + vвҲ’, (D.11) can be rewritten as
g(вҲҮSe1 (v+ + vвҲ’), e1) + g(вҲҮSe2
(v+ + vвҲ’), e2) = вҲ’гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n , nгҖү . (D.12)
Notice now that the left hand side of (D.12) is the divergence in S of the tangent vector field v+ + vвҲ’, namely we have
divS (v+ + vвҲ’) = вҲ’гҖҲD(v+ + vвҲ’) В· n , nгҖү . (D.13)
Next, by Gauss theorem, the fact that S is has a smooth boundary implies that ЛҶ
S divS (v+ + vвҲ’) = вҲ’
ЛҶ*вҲӮS*(v+ + vвҲ’) В·*ОҪ*, (D.14)
where we denote by*ОҪ*the smooth unit vector field on*вҲӮS*which is tangent to S , orthogonal to*вҲӮS*and points вҖңinwardsвҖқ, i.e. towards S . Since v++ vвҲ’
is parallel to*вҲӮS*, the integrand in the right hand side of (D.14) vanishes identically, which allows us to conclude (D.10).
249
Appendix*D.2.*Rigidity of the CVS condition for closed surfaces We prove rigidity results for closed connected CVS surfaces without
imposing the stability condition (D.6). We prove that
(A) there are no CVS solutions with S homeomorphic to the sphere;
(B) if S has genus bigger than 1, then S cannot be real analytic;
(C) if S has genus 1, then there are no axisymmetric solutions.
The precise formulation of (A) is the following:
**Theorem Appendix D.6.**If (vвҲ’, v+) satisfies the CVS conditions (D.1), (D.2), (D.3), (D.4), (D.5), and S is homeomorphic to the sphere, then v+ = 0 and vвҲ’ = 0.
Point (B) is a consequence of a more general fact which we state in the following theorem.
**Theorem Appendix D.7.**The following holds:
(i) If S is a smooth closed connected CVS surface with genus different than 1, then |v+|2 = |vвҲ’|2.
(ii) If S is any smooth CVS surface and |v+|2 = |vвҲ’|2, then for any point q вҲҲ S one of the following conditions must necessarily hold:
(a) The Gauss curvature of S is 0 at q;
(b) v+(q) = vвҲ’(q);
(c) v+(q) = вҲ’vвҲ’(q).
From the above theorem we draw the following simple
**Corollary Appendix D.8.**Assume S is a closed smooth connected CVS surface with genus strictly higher than 1. Then either v+ = vвҲ’ on some nontrivial open subset of S , or v+ = вҲ’vвҲ’ on some nontrivial open subset of S .
Proof. In fact, if the sets {v+ = vвҲ’} and {v+ = вҲ’vвҲ’} contain no interior points, then the Gauss curvature would vanish on a dense set, and by continuity it must vanish anywhere. But it is well known that a surface with vanishing Gauss curvature cannot be closed.
250
The condition is even more stringent if S is real analytic, i.e. it can be described as the graph of a function with a converging Taylor series around any point, up to rotation of the coordinates.
**Corollary Appendix D.9.**Assume S is a closed real analytic connected CVS surface with genus strictly higher than 1. Then either v+ = vвҲ’ on S , or v+ = вҲ’vвҲ’ on S .
Proof. Since both v+ and vвҲ’ are locally gradients of harmonic functions which satisfy the Neumann boundary condition, it turns out that their restrictions to S are real analytic as well. But real analytic functions which vanish on a nontrivial open set, must vanish identically because S is connected.
We finally detail the non existence result (C) for axisymmetric solutions of genus 1. Let us consider a closed simple curve [0, 1] 3 t вҶ’*Оі(t)*=*(Оіr(t), Оіz(t))*вҲҲ (0, вҲһ)2, and the associated torus of rotation S вҠӮ**R3,**parametrized by
(t,*Оё)вҶ’ (Оіr(t)*cos*Оё, Оіr(t)*sin*Оё, Оіz(t))*. (D.15)
We use polar coordinates (x, y, z) = (r cos*Оё,*r sin*Оё,*z). Recall that*вҲӮ вҲӮr*, 1
r*вҲӮ вҲӮОё*,
*вҲӮ вҲӮz*is an orthonormal frame.
We look for CVS solutions with axisymmetry. The general Ansatz is the following.
vвҲ’ :=*Оұ*1 r2
*вҲӮ*

for some*Оұ*вҲҲ**R**and C1 functions a, b and c.
**Theorem Appendix D.10.**Let S be given by (D.15), for some simple closed curve*Оі*=*(Оіr, Оіz)*of class C2. If (vвҲ’, v+) is an axisymmetric pair that satisfies the CVS conditions (D.1), (D.2), (D.3), (D.4), (D.5), then either v+ = vвҲ’ on S , or v+ = вҲ’vвҲ’ on S .

Appendix*D.3.*Proof of Theorem Appendix*D.6*Since SвҲ’ is simply connected we can write vвҲ’ = вҲҮОҰвҲ’ for some har-
monic function ОҰвҲ’, whose gradient is tangent to S . A simple integration by parts gives
ЛҶ SвҲ’ |vвҲ’|2 dx =
ЛҶ SвҲ’ |вҲҮОҰвҲ’|2 dx =
ЛҶ S
ОҰвҲ’вҲҮОҰвҲ’ В· n dx = 0 , (D.16)
where n denotes the exterior normal to S . We deduce that vвҲ’ = 0. Thanks to Theorem Appendix D.7(i) we deduce that v+ = 0 on S . Since v+ is harmonic, the unique continuation principle implies that v+ vanishes identically.
Appendix*D.4.*Proof of Theorem Appendix*D.7*Let us begin by proving (i). A well-known theorem in topology implies
that any tangent vector field to a closed surface S with genus different than 1 must necessarily vanish at some point. This excludes that the constant in (D.5) is positive (because then v+ would never vanish) or negative (because then vвҲ’ would never vanish).
Let us pass to the proof of (ii). The key ingredient is the following lemma, whose proof is postponed at the end of this section.
**Lemma Appendix D.11.**Let ОЈ вҠӮ**R3**be a smooth surface and let U вҠӮ ОЈ be an open set. Assume the existence of smooth velocity fields u, w defined on U and tangent to ОЈ. If
(i) u(p), w(p) 6= 0 and gp(v, w) = 0 for any p вҲҲ U, where g is the metric induced by the ambient space**R3;**
(ii) [u, w] = 0 in U;
(iii) u and v are gradients in U, i.e. there exist*Оұ, ОІ*: U вҶ’**R**such that gp(u, В·) =*dpОұ*and gp(v, В·) =*dpОІ*for any p вҲҲ U.
Then, the Gaussian curvature of ОЈ is zero in U.
Let us explain how to prove Theorem Appendix D.7(ii) given Lemma Appendix D.11. Fix q such that v+(q) 6= vвҲ’(q) and v+(q) 6= vвҲ’(q) and let U be a neighborhood of q where v+ вҲ’ vвҲ’ and v+ + vвҲ’ never vanish. Since |v+|2 = |vвҲ’|2, we have gp(u, w) = 0 for any p вҲҲ U. Theorem Appendix
252
D.1 implies that [v+, vвҲ’] = 0, hence [u, w] = 0 in U. Moreover, (D.2) imply that v+ and vвҲ’ are irrotational in S+ and SвҲ’, respectively, and (D.3) says that v+ and vвҲ’ are tangent to S . This implies that, v+ and vвҲ’ are locally gradients in S . In particular u and w are locally gradients in U. We are in position to apply Lemma Appendix D.11 with ОЈ = S , which implies that K = 0 in U, where K denotes the Gaussian curvature of S . This implies in particular that the Gauss curvature of S vanishes at q.

Appendix*D.6.*Solutions with cylindrical symmetry In21 Migdal finds stable solutions to the CVS equations with linear
growth and vorticity concentrated on a cylinder S вҠӮ**R3.**It turns out that the cross section of S is noncompact. Given the cylindrical symmetry and the linear growth at infinity, that is the best one can hope for. Below we show that there are no solutions to the CVS equations (irrespectively of the stability condition) with cylindrical symmetry, compact cross section and linear growth.
Consider:
(i) a smooth simple closed curve*Пғ*вҠӮ**R2;**
(ii) the bounded simply connected domain О©вҲ’ bounded by*Пғ;*
(iii) the unbounded domain О©+ :=**R2**\*(Пғ*вҲӘО©вҲ’);
(iv) the surface S вҠӮ**R3**given by**ПғГ—R;**
(v) the cylindrical domains SВұ := О©Вұ**Г—R.**
We are looking for two bounded vector fields vВұ : О©Вұ вҶ’**R2**and a quadratic function q :**R3**вҶ’**R**with the following properties:
(a) q is harmonic;
(b) the maps uВұ := (vВұ, 0) +вҲҮq : SВұ вҶ’**R3**are divergence free, curl-free and tangent to S ;
(c) for any point p вҲҲ S , the vector u+(p)вҲ’ uвҲ’(p) belongs to the kernel of Du+(p) + DuвҲ’(p).
258
We claim the following.
**Theorem Appendix D.13.**vВұ and q must vanish identically.
We can in fact consider the following more general situation:
(iвҖҷ)*Пғi,*i вҲҲ {1, . . . , N0}, is an arbitrary finite collection of simple closed curves which are pairwise disjoint;
(iiвҖҷ) {О©j}, j вҲҲ {1, . . . , N0 + 1} are the connected components of**R2**\вӢғi*Пғi;*
(iiiвҖҷ) The surface S вҠӮ**R3**is the union of the cylinders Si :=*Пғi***Г—R;**
(ivвҖҷ) The cylindrical domains are given by Оӣj := О©j**Г—R.**
Under these more general assumptions we are looking for N0 + 1 bounded vector fields vj : О©j вҶ’**R2**and a quadratic function q :**R3**вҶ’**R**with the following properties:
(aвҖҷ) q is harmonic;
(bвҖҷ) The maps uj := (vj, 0) +вҲҮq : Оӣj вҶ’**R3**are divergence-free, curl-free, and tangent to*вҲӮОӣj*вҠӮ S ;
(cвҖҷ) If Оӣi and Оӣj have a common boundary Sk, then for any point p вҲҲ Sk the vector ui(p)вҲ’ uj(p) belongs to the kernel of Dui(p) + Duj(p).
Under these assumptions Theorem Appendix D.13 can be generalized to
**Theorem Appendix D.14.**vi and q must vanish identically.
Proof of Theorem Appendix*D.13.*Consider the function*П•(x,*y) = q(x, y, 0) and the vector field*ОҫвҲ’(x,*y) = vвҲ’(x,*y)+вҲҮП•(x,*y). Observe that vвҲ’ is curl-free and, since О©вҲ’ is simply connected, there is a potential*О¶вҲ’*: О©вҲ’ вҶ’**R**
for*ОҫвҲ’.*Now,*вҲӮО¶вҲ’ вҲӮОҪ*
= 0 on*Пғ*=*вҲӮО©вҲ’.*(D.31)
On the other hand, since*П•*is quadratic,*вҲҶО¶вҲ’*is a constant. Observe that, therefore, from ЛҶ
О©вҲ’*вҲҶО¶вҲ’*=
ЛҶ*Пғ*
*вҲӮО¶вҲ’ вҲӮОҪ*
= 0 , (D.32)
259
it turns out that*вҲҶО¶вҲ’*=*вҲҶП•*= 0 . (D.33)
But then we can integrate by parts to conclude ЛҶ
О©вҲ’*|вҲҮО¶вҲ’|2*=

= 0 . (D.34)
In particular*ОҫвҲ’*vanishes identically. Define next*Оҫ+(x,*y) = v+(x, y)*+вҲҮП•(x,*y). (c) implies that*Оҫ+(x,*y)
is in the kernel of*DОҫ+(x,*y) for every (x, y) вҲҲ*Пғ.*Assume*О¶+*is a potential for*Оҫ+*in some simply connected domain U вҲ©О©+, where U is the neighborhood of some point (x, y) вҲҲ*Пғ.*Then the latter condition can be rewritten as
1 2*вҲҮ|вҲҮО¶+|2*= 0 on*Пғ*вҲ©U .
If*|вҲҮО¶+|*= 0 on*Пғ*вҲ©U, then*О¶+*is a constant over*Пғ*вҲ©U and if we extend*О¶+*to О©вҲ’ вҲ©U by setting it equal to the latter constant, we immediately see that*О¶+*is C1 on U and weakly harmonic, hence harmonic. So*О¶+*must vanish by unique continuation for harmonic functions. If*|вҲҮО¶+|*= c > 0, we conclude that*D2О¶+(p)*has a nontrivial kernel for every p вҲҲ*Пғ*вҲ©U, but since the trace of the two-dimensional matrix*D2О¶+(p)*is zero, we must conclude that*D2О¶+*vanishes identically on*Пғ*вҲ©U. But this means that*вҲӮО¶+*
*вҲӮx*and*вҲӮО¶+*

on*Пғ*вҲ©U implies therefore that*вҲҮО¶+*must vanish identically on*Пғ*вҲ©U. Having concluded that both*ОҫвҲ’*and*Оҫ+*vanish identically, we imme-
diately conclude that actually u+ = uвҲ’ on S and that the corresponding function u given by defining u = uВұ on SВұ is the gradient of a quadratic harmonic function. Since u must be tangent to S , we see right away that u must vanish identically.
Proof of Theorem Appendix*D.14.*The proof is by induction over the number N0 of curves. The start of the the induction, namely N0 = 1, is in fact Theorem Appendix D.13. Consider therefore an arbitrary N0 > 1 and assume that the theorem is correct when N0 is substituted by N0 вҲ’ 1. Fix a collection of curves*Пғi*as in (iвҖҷ) above. Each*Пғi*bounds a unique simply connected domain Оһi in**R2,**and given that the curves are pairwise disjoint, each*Пғj*with j 6= i is either contained in Оһi or in the interior of its complement. Since the curves are finitely many it is obvious that one of
260
them is an вҖңinnermostвҖқ curve, namely there is a Оһi which does not contain any curve*Пғj.*Without loss of generality we can assume i = 1 and observe that Оһ1 must be one of the domains О©i: again without loss of generality we can assume it is О©1. We then denote by О©2 the only other connected component of**R2**\ вӢғi*Пғi*whose boundary intersects*Пғ1*(in fact we must have*Пғ1*вҠӮ*вҲӮО©2,*but observe that the inclusion might be strict). If we set vвҲ’ := v1 and v+ := v2, we can now repeat the argument of Theorem Appendix D.13. First of all the potential*О¶вҲ’*exists in our case as well because О©1 is simply connected, and hence the conclusions (D.31)-(D.32) can all be drawn in our case as well. The subsequent argument leads to the conclusion that v+ is in fact a smooth continuation of vвҲ’ across*Пғ1*вҲ©U in any simply connected neighborhood U of p вҲҲ*Пғ*=*Пғ1:*the argument can be taken verbatim in our case as long as U does not intersect any other curve*Пғj*with j > 1. In particular we conclude that*Пғ1*could actually be eliminated from the collection of curves because the function б№Ҫ defined to be v1 in О©1 and v2 in О©2 is in fact smooth across*Пғ1.*Having reduced the number of curves by 1 we can apply the inductive assumption and conclude the validity of the theorem.
261
**Appendix E. Topological defects in Turbulent flows, (by Grigory Volovik)**
262
Let us consider the Migdal view on classical turbulence (see Refs.18, 9) and look for the connections between the classical hydrodynamics and turbulence in liquids and quantum hydrodynamics and turbulence in fermionic superfluids.
Appendix*E.1.*Classical vs quantum turbulence Of course, there are important differences between the turbulence in
quantum and classical systems. In classical liquids, the viscous term has more derivatives that nonlinear Euler term. In fermionic superfluids both terms have the same number of derivatives, see Eq.(2) in Ref.64
The corresponding Reynolds number is the ratio of two dimensionless parameters вҖ“ the internal parameters of the liquid, reactive coefficient 1вҲ’*ОұвҖІ*and the dissipative one*Оұ.*This Reynolds number does not depend on velocity: it depends only on such internal characteristic of the liquid as temperature T. By changing T, the transition to quantum turbulence is experimentally observed, when the ratio of the reactive and dissipative parameters crosses unity.
The possible relation of viscosity anomaly to chiral anomaly may follow from the observation that the parameters*Оұ*and*ОұвҖІ*in Eq.(2) of Ref.64 are related to the Adler-Bell-Jackiw (ABJ) chiral anomaly. The vortex has chirality, and its motion produces the spectral flow in the vortex cores, which is similar to that in ABJ anomaly and which in some limit case is described by the ABJ equation.65 In that limit, which takes place for vorticity in chiral superfluid 3He-A, the ABJ equation with the proper prefactor has been experimentally confirmed.66
Appendix*E.2.*Clebsch hydrodynamics vs Heisenberg ferromagnets The velocity field in Clebsch variable*(ПҶ1, ПҶ2, ПҶ3)*is vi =*вҲ’ПҶ2вҲҮiПҶ1*+
*вҲҮiПҶ3.*In the Migdal theory, velocity and vorticity are determined in the compact space S2, see Eq.(43) for vorticity in terms of the unit vector**Ең:**
*Пүik*= (вҲҮГ—**v)ik**=**Z(Ең**В·**(вҲҮiЕңГ—вҲҮkЕң))**. (E.1)
The polar and azimuthal components of the unit vector**Ең**are*ПҶ1/Z*= cos*Оё, ПҶ1*=*ПҶ,*while Z is adiabatic invariant in the absence of viscosity.
Eq.(E.1) also takes place in chiral superfluid 3He-A, where vorticity is expressed in terms of the unit vector of the orbital angular momentum**lМӮ**вүЎ**Ең.**In this superfluid the prefactor Z is fixed, Z = hМ„/4m3, where m3 is the mass of the 3He atom,67, 65 and this equation is known as the
263
Mermin-Ho relation. Since in the chiral superfluid*Оә*=*ПҖhМ„/m3*is the the quantum of circulation, the prefactor Z in Migdal hydrodynamics plays the role of the circulation quantum, see Sec.Appendix E.4.
However, the Clebsch fields are not well determined, since*ПҶ2*is not defined at*Оё*= 0. Actually these Clebsch variables are exactly the same as used in ferromagnets, where the magnetization is**M**=**MmМӮ**вүЎ**ZЕң,**M вҲ’ Mz = M(1вҲ’ cos*ОІ)*вүЎ Z(1вҲ’ cos*ОІ)*=*ПҶ1,*and*ПҶ*вүЎ*ПҶ2.*Here*ОІ*is the angle of deflection of magnetization from the z-axis, and*ПҶ*is the azimuthal angle of magnetization. The role of vorticity is played by the effective gauge field Fik вүЎ*Пүik*acting on electrons from magnetization.68
The pseudoelectric field Fi0 вүЎ*Пүi0*gives rise to the so-called spin-motive force acting on electrons. These gauge fields are related to the Berry phase.
In ferromagnets,*ПҶ*and Mz (or M вҲ’ Mz, since M is the dynamical invariant) are canonically conjugate variables. But since*ПҶ*вүЎ*ПҶ2*is not well determined, the 3 + 1 action
Вҙ*dtdVMzПҶМҮ*is substituted by the 4 + 1 Wess-
Zumino term, Вҙ
*dtdП„dVF0П„,*see Eq.(19) in Ref.68 Probably such approach is to be used in Migdal theory.
One may say that the big difference with ferromagnets is that there the magnetization is observable, while in hydrodynamics the Clebsch variables are not. However, if the spin-orbit interaction is neglected, the magnetization becomes unobservable. But the effective gauge invariant gauge field*FВөОҪ*acting on fermions in ferromagnets will be observable in the same way as its analog вҖ“ gauge invariant vorticity*ПүВөОҪ,*which is expressed in terms of the unit vector**Ең**of the analog of magnetization.
Appendix*E.3.*Topological objects: hedgehog, instanton, domain wall, quantum of circulation
Since M вүЎ Z is the dynamical invariant in reversible dynamics, the reversible hydrodynamics is fully determined by the unit vector of magnetization**mМӮ**вүЎ**Ең.**It has two topological invariants*ПҖ2*and*ПҖ3.*
The*ПҖ2*invariant is N = Вҙ
*dSiПү*i/Z. It is proportional to the velocity
circulation along the moving loop, which is conserved in moving liquid in the absence of dissipation. For the closed surface, the invariant N should be integer describing the topological defects вҖ“ the hedgehogs or monopoles. In ferromagnets, where**mМӮ**is the original variable and vorticity (gauge field*Пүik)*is the secondary variable, the hedgehog in**mМӮ**field looks as the Dirac monopole in the Berry phase, with the unobservable Dirac line, at which the Berry phase changes by*2ПҖ.*
264
In hydrodynamics, where vorticity*Пүik*is the original variable and**Ең**is the secondary one, the hedgehogs in the**Ең**field are impossible as separate objects. The Dirac line attached to such monopole is singular and is observable. The same is in the chiral superfluids, where the Dirac line is the singular doubly quantized vortex, m = 2 (or m = 2N in general case, where N is the topological charge of the monopole). Nevertheless, in Migdal theory the integer m is important, because the circulation О“ = Zm, and thus the prefactor Z plays the role of circulation quantum, and m is the number of quanta.
The*ПҖ3*invariant is the Hopf invariant, which is proportional to the integral over helicity: N3 =
Вҙ d3r**v**В·Пү/Z2. The corresponding topological
defect is instanton, which describes the process of creation of helicity from the "vacuum".
In Heisenberg ferromagnets there are no domain walls, because*ПҖ0*= 0. In hydrodynamics the surfaces are possible at which the prefactor Z changes sign. Probably the vortex sheets in Migdal theory вҖ“ the thin pancake-like regions of increasing vorticity69 are the analogous objects.
Appendix*E.4.*Dynamic invariants in hydrodynamics (circulation and helicity) and their quantization in superfluids
In Migdal theory, the circulation О“ = Zm is the dynamic invariant. In superfluids, the quantity m becomes the integer number, i.e. the circulation is quantized: Z =*2ПҖhМ„/m4*and Z =*ПҖhМ„/m3*is the quantum of circulations in Bose superfluid 4He with mass of atom m4 and in Fermi superfluid 3He with mass of atom m3 correspondingly, and m is the number circulation quanta. Thus in Migdal theory, the prefactor Z plays the role of circulation quantum, and m plays the role of the number of quanta, which becomes integer in the quantum case.
In the same manner, in superfluids the integral of helicity is quantized, H = Z2N3 with integer N3. The integer N3 in superfluids is the topological invariant: it is the knot invariant in Bose superfluid 4He and in Fermi superfluid 3He-B, and the Hopf invariant*ПҖ3(S2)*in the chiral superfluid 3He-A. In both Fermi superfluids, the quantum of helicity is Z2 вҲқ (hМ„/m3)
2. In classical hydrodynamics, the prefactor Z2 вҲқ (hМ„/m3) 2
serves as the "quantum of helicity", and N3 serves as the "number of quanta", which becomes the integer topological invariant in the quantum case of superfluids.
265
The quantization of helicity and of circulation in superfluids provide two examples, which illustrate the main property of the dynamical invariants in classical systems. The dynamic invariant in classical systems become quantized in integer numbers in the quantum systems, N3 and N correspondingly.
Similar situation is in Heisenberg ferromagnets. In classical equations M вүЎ Z is the dynamic invariant, which is conserved in the LandauвҖ“Lifshitz equation without dissipation. In ferromagnets the total magnetic momentВҙ
d3xM becomes quantized in terms of hМ„/2, i.e. Вҙ
d3xM = khМ„/2, where k is integer. This quantization follows from the Wess-Zumino term in action.70
Appendix*E.5.*Hydrodynamics in terms of tetrads Let us introduce the orthogonal triad of unit length:
**e1**,**e2**,**Ең**=**e1**Г—**e2**. (E.2)
The velocity field:
vi =**Z(e1**В·**вҲҮie2**вҲ’**e2**В·**вҲҮie1)**, (E.3)
where Z is the dynamical invariant. The vorticity is expressed in terms**Ең**field (according to the Mermin-Ho relation) in the same way as in Migdal theory in Eq.(43) of Ref.:18
*Пүik*= (вҲҮГ—**v)ik**=**Z(Ең**В·**(вҲҮiЕңГ—вҲҮkЕң))**. (E.4)
The triad field variables correspond to three Clebsch fields, but as distinct from the Clebsch fields the triad is well determined everywhere, except for the real topological singularities.
The SO(3) space of triad, and S2 space of**Ең**provide the geometry, including the effective metric, and topology. The topological invariants are well determined in terms of**Ең,**or in terms of rotation of the dyad about**Ең-axis.**They are integer for special geometries, while the circulation and helicity are represented by these invariants multiplied by the dynamical invariants Z and Z2 correspondingly.
266
Appendix*E.6.*Dirac monopole with observable Dirac strings The hydrodynamics of incompressible fluid has been discussed in
terms of the**S-vector**in Ref.71 The unit vector description allows the topological objects*ПҖ3(S2)*= Z, which describe the Hopf structure, and also the instantons. It also allows the defects of the group*ПҖ2(S2)*= Z, the hedgehogs, or monopoles, in which the**S-vector**wraps N times around a sphere. In tetrads, where the target space is SO(3), the Hopf structures are allowed, since*ПҖ3(SO(3))*= Z. But the hedgehogs do not exist as separate objects, since*ПҖ2(SO(3))*= 0. That is why the monopole in**S-field**has singular Dirac string (the vortex singularity) attached to the monopole, which is observable.72, 73, 74
The simplest monopole is the radial hedgehog in the**Ең**field:**Ең**=**rМӮ.**Its topological*ПҖ2*charge is N = 1. Let us consider two configurations of N = 1 monopole: (i) with one Dirac string with winding number m = 2N = 2; and (ii) with two strings, each with winding number m = 1.
Appendix*E.7.*Dirac monopole with one Dirac string (z,*ПҒ, ПҶ)*are cylindrical coordinates and (r,*Оё, ПҶ)*are spherical coordi-
nates. The velocity field is
**v(r)**= ZПҶМӮ 1вҲ’ cos*Оё*
r sin*Оё*, вҲҮ В·**v**= 0 , (E.5)
and vorticity field:
**Пү(r)**= Z**rМӮ**r2 +**2ZОё(вҲ’z)бә‘Оҙ2(ПҒ)**, вҲҮ В·Пү = 0 . (E.6)
The vorticity is equivalent to magnetic field of Dirac monopole with charge Z, i.e.**B**вүЎ**Пү(r).**The magnetic flux is conserved, вҲҮ В·**B**= 0. The*4ПҖZ*flux from the monopole is compensated by the magnetic flux along the singular flux tube (observable Dirac string) to the monopole. Here there is the single Dirac string on the lower half-axis, z < 0. It is the vortex line with circulation 2Z, or winding number m = 2 in Migdal notations. The triads are
**Ең(r)**=**rМӮ**,**e1**+**ie2**=*eiПҶ(ОёМӮ+*iПҶМӮ) . (E.7)
This topological structure with vortex terminated by monopole may have some connection to tornado, see Figs. E.46 and E.47. Here the funnel cloud moves towards the ground and when it reaches the ground it forms
267
tornado in Fig.E.48. Before the tornado is formed the funnel cloud is the vortex with the end point. The end point represents the monopolehedgehog in the**Ең-field**and the funnel cloud represents its Dirac string. Probably the monopole may exist also at the final stage of the tornado: when tornado is dying the end of the vortex leaves the ground.
Appendix*E.8.*Dirac monopole with two Dirac strings In this case the velocity field is:
**v(r)**= вҲ’ZПҶМӮ cos*Оё*
r sin*Оё*, (E.8)
and vorticity field:
**Пү(r)**= Z**rМӮ**r2 + Z sign(z)**бә‘Оҙ2(ПҒ)**. (E.9)
Now the flux from the monopole is compensated by the magnetic fluxes which enter the monopole along two Dirac strings. Each string (vortex line) has circulation Z, or winding number m = 1 in Migdal notations. The triads are
**Ең(r)**=**rМӮ**,**e1**+**ie2**= ОёМӮ+ iПҶМӮ . (E.10)
Appendix*E.9.*General case with two Dirac strings and monopole with charge N The monopole in**S-field**with topological charge N, has the Dirac
strings with the total winding number m = 2N. The triads can be chosen as extension of m = 1 monopole in Eq.(E.10):
**e1**+**ie2**= (ОёМӮ+ iПҶМӮ)N . (E.11)
There are two Dirac strings, at z < 0 and at z > 0, each with winding number m = N (the total winding number is m = 2N). It is not clear whether the monopoles with |N| > 1 could be realized.
Appendix*E.10.*Monopole in spherical layer The monopole configuration with N = 1 with two strings, each with
m = 1, is realized for hydrodynamics of ideal incompressible fluid in a thin spherical layer.75 Here**S**=**rМӮ,**and thus the topological charge of the monopole is N = 1. The first term in the RHS of Eq.(E.9) can be neglected

вҲҮ В·**v**= 0 , вҲҮГ—**(vГ—Пү)**= 0 . (E.12)

Appendix*E.11.*From monopole to pancake vortex sheet Now we can adiabatically deform the inner sphere, so that the topo-
logical invariant N = 1 is conserved. Then the flow will be automatically adjusted to the new configuration of the core.
There are several interesting deformations with the conservation of axial symmetry.
One may adiabatically decrease the radius of the core. In the limit of small radius the configuration will represent the monopole with the singular core.
One may deform the inner sphere to the disk of small thickness without violating the axial symmetry and the discrete symmetry with respect to inversion:**S(r)**=**вҲ’S(вҲ’r), v(r)**=**v(вҲ’r), Пү(r)**=**вҲ’Пү(вҲ’r).**This would result in the Migdal vortex sheet.
So, let us consider the monopole structure obtained when the inner sphere in Sec.Appendix E.10 is adiabatically (i.e. with conservation of topological invariant N = 1) deformed to the thin disk. In the vicinity of the disk one has Sz(z) = |Sz| sign(z), i.e. the disk represents the domain wall, where Sz changes sign, but*|Sz(ПҒ)|*is continuous across the sheet. Velocity is**v**=**ZSzвҲҮПҶ2(r).**Let us first consider**ПҶ2(r)**=*ПҶ2(ПҶ)*=*ПҶ,*

This structure represents the monopole with N = 1 and two Dirac strings, each with winding number m = 1. The core of the monopole is represented by the disk. On two sides of the disk one has the flow circulating in opposite directions. The singular contribution*Оҙ2(ПҒ)*to vorticity along z axis represents two Dirac strings with opposite circulations. These Dirac strings enter the monopole (vortex sheet disk) from two sides.
Appendix*E.12.*Wall bounded by string In the present scenario, the disk itself represents the Z2 domain wall
at which Sz changes sign. In the body of the disk, the vector**ЕңвҠҘ**has*2ПҖn*rotations across the disk. In the Migdal scenario the vector ~SвҠҘ changes sign., and it makes half-integer number of rotations (2n +*1)ПҖ,*or, a reflection in addition to*2ПҖn*rotations.18, 9
In both cases the edge of the disk represents the circular string (ring), which terminates the domain wall. This string has either the*ПҖ1*winding
272
![image](https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/notebooklm/AKXwDQGniMYlUtJQ8-kJHmzscdqwVIxl8MolPp1OxS3C8npbhxPkzlGBtx9nb5ENoXttRCumoyqXDi0TOdrRYUsx8yJGA91ZnHLOFamh_5uxF7ZgQHZt8YIFqkHmRQ06kv05vWyRKsufTQ=w274-h170-v0?authuser=0)

number n, or the winding number n + 1/2. In the first case it is the analog of the soliton terminated by strings,76 while in the second case it is the analog of Kibble-Lazarides-Shafi (KLS) wall77, 78 bounded by Alice strings. The similar configuration, the domain wall bounded by Alice string (half-quantum vortex), has been experimentally studied in the polar phase of superfluid 3He.79 The general topology of the KLS walls bounded by string was discussed in Ref.80 In both cases, solitons terminated by strings and domain walls terminated by strings, the classification is in terms of the relative homotopy groups. The winding number n or n + 1/2 is the element of topology of such combined objects.
These combined objects are described by the combination of topologies. This includes*ПҖ2(S2)*= Z (the topological charge N = 2m of the hedhehogmonopole and the winding number of Dirac strings, which enter the monopole);*ПҖ1(S1)*= Z (the winding number n of the soliton and of the circular ring, which terminates the soliton);*ПҖ0(Z2)*= Z2 (the domain wall).
Appendix*E.13. SO(3)*hydrodynamics of incompressible liquid Let us introduce instead of the triads with unit length**ea,**the more
general triads**Ea:**
**E1**=**Ae1**,**E2**=**Ae2**,**L**=**E3**=**E1**Г—**E2**=**Ze3**вүЎ**ZS**, (E.15)
**E2**1 =**E2**
2 = A2 = Z ,**E1**В·**E2**= 0 . (E.16)
The velocity field is: vi =**E1**В·**вҲҮiE2**. (E.17)
Note that this is the general form of velocity, which is not violated by the condition of incompressibility. The latter condition is obtained by gauge transformation in Eq.(E.25).
We have the following PB for canonically conjugate variables:

The Eq.(E.21) gives the following motion equations for the tetrad fields**Ea,**with a = 1, 2, 3:
**Д–a**+**(v**В·**вҲҮ)Ea**= 0 , (E.22)
Multiplying each of 3 equations to the corresponding**Ea**one obtains that each of three equation gives the equation for Z:
Е» +**(v**В· вҲҮ)Z = 0 . (E.23)
This demonstrates that Z can be chosen as constant in spacetime. The PB for velocity field are:
**{vi(r1), vk(r2)}**=*eiklПү***l(r1)Оҙ(r2**вҲ’**r1)**. (E.24)
Gauge transformation which does not change the distribution of vector**L**(and the vorticity, which is expressed in terms of**L),**is:
**E1**+**iE2**вҶ’**(E1**+**iE2)eiОҰ**,**vвҶ’ v**+вҲҮОҰ . (E.25)
This is the rotation of the dyad**E1**and**E2**by angle ОҰ about the local direction of**L-vector.**The gauge transformation is needed to satisfy the incompressibility condition вҲҮ В·**v**= 0. It is analogous to transformation from 3 Clebsch variables to 2. But as distinct from Clebsch variables, triads correctly deal with with such structures, where Clebsch variables have unphysical singularities (for example in the configuration with monopole and Dirac strings in Sec.Appendix E.6).

Appendix*E.15.*Conclusion It looks that not only the relativistic quantum field theories, but also
the quantum hydrodynamics of topological superfluids can be useful for study the important structures emerging in classical liquids.
